INDIA
Uttar Pradesh Fiscal Reform and Public Sector Restructuring Credit/Loan Summary
Borrower:             Government of India
Implementing Agency: Government of Uttar Pradesh, Office of the Chief Secretary and Departments
of Finance, Planning, Personnel and Administrative Reforms
Beneficiaries:       State of Uttar Pradesh (population: 160 million)
Amount:              SDR 93.3 million (US$125 million equivalent) in IDA credit and
US$126.3 million in IBRD loan
Terms:               IDA: Standard with 35 years maturity and 10 years grace period
IBRD: Payable in 20 years including 5 years of grace period; standard variable
interest rates for LIBOR based USD single currency loans
Onlending Terms:     Standard terms of central resource transfer for state developmental budgets.
Description:         Uttar Pradesh is India's most populous state and one of its poorest. It has
experienced prolonged economic stagnation, fiscal crisis, and little progress
against its massive poverty (41% poor) in recent decades. The Uttar Pradesh
Fiscal Reform and Public Sector Restructuring Credit/Loan would support the
government's efforts in initiating multi-year reforms to turn this situation
around. The fiscal reform component of the program is aimed at ensuring
sustainability and to create additional fiscal space over the medium term for
well-targeted public investments in high priority areas of development,
including quantitative and qualitative improvement in social sector spending.
The public sector restructuring component of the reform program is intended
to improve the quality of service delivery by reorienting the role of
government, by reforming the civil service, by reducing corruption through
improved transparency, deregulation and strengthening of anti-corruption
institutions, by decentralization and by improving financial management and
accountability. The proposed credit/loan will be disbursed in one tranche, and
is the first in a proposed sequence of three or four operations. The completed
actions triggering the presentation of this operation to the Board have been
agreed upon and emerged from the economic policy dialogue with GoUP
spanning the last 18 months.
iBenefits:           The operation, which is designed to support CAS objectives and is a key
component of the Bank's overall program of assistance in UP, reinforces recent
initiatives by the Government of India to stimulate reforms in the states.
Expected direct benefits include: (a) improved fiscal situation; (b) improved
governance and social service delivery; (c) higher economic growth and
reduced poverty; (d) contribution to reduction in the overall public sector
borrowing requirement; and (e) encouragement of similar reforms in other
Indian states.
Risks:               The major risks are: (a) possible reversibility of reforms due to political
resistance; and (b) lack of adequate institutional capacity.
Poverty Category:    NA
Disbursement:        The credit/loan would be disbursed in one tranche in an amount equivalent to
US$251.3 million upon effectiveness. Key actions agreed over the past 18
months of dialogue with the government have already been taken.
Project ID Number:   IN-PE 65471

March 15, 2000
Mr. E. A. S. Sarma
Secretary, Department of Economic Affairs
Government of India
Ministry of Finance
North Block, Delhi
India
Dear Mr. Sarma:
Subject: Proposed Fiscal Reform and Public Sector Restructurng LoanJCredit
Letter of Development Policy
Introduction
1.   Uttar Pradesh (UP) is a state with a proud history and significant accomplishments to its credit.
However, over the last decade, slowing growth, serious fiscal problems, and a weakening of public
institutions have constrained the economic and social development of our state. In particular, as a result of
our fiscal and institutional problems, the quality and quantity of public services rendered through health,
education, nutrition, population and anti-poverty programs have suffered. Poverty, significantly above
the national average at around 41 percent, is stagnating.
2.    The Govermment of Uttar Pradesh is making a concerted effort to revitalize the state's economic
performance through a wide-ranging reform program. We are strengthening the provision of education
and health services to improve the basic well-being of our people. We are restructuring the major
infrastructure sectors to attract private investment, and accelerate growth. In particular, we have initiated
a comprehensive restructuring of the power sector, which until now has been a drain on our finances and
the most severe constraint on our growth. We have now passed reform legislation and established an
independent regulator. We have unbundled the State Electricity Board into separate companies in
preparation for privatization.
3.    However, all of these and related efforts will not bear fruit unless we restore soundness to the
state's fiscal position, and to the quality of governance. In order to improve both the quality and quantity
of essential public services, it is critical that the state's high fiscal deficit is reduced while high priority
development and social expenditures are increased. System-wide improvements in the incentives,
processes, and public institutions are needed to redefine the role of the government in the economy
through deregulation and divestment while increasing its transparency, efficiency and accountability.
4.    In continuation of our ongoing discussions with Gol on fiscal reforms, we have developed a wide-
ranging medium-term Fiscal Reform and Public Sector Restructuring Program (the Program), described
below. The Government has already taken a number of difficult measures, during the 1999-2000 fiscal
year, in the areas of fiscal stabilization, structural reforns and administrative improvements. As a result,
the deterioration in the state's finances has been arrested and revenue collection, expenditure control and
financial management have all started to improve.
5.    To implement and ensure adequate funding for the costs associated with the first phase of our
Program, the Government of Uttar Pradesh (the Government) would like the Government of India to
propose to the World Bank for consideration a Fiscal Reform and Public Sector Restructuring
Loan/Credit of about Rs. 1100 crore (US$250 million equivalent), as the first in a potential series of three
or four such operations. This assistance would provide financing of the transitional costs of reform. It
would supplement the assistance we are receiving from the Government of India to support our Reform
Program and project assistance we are proposing to the World Bank in the social sectors, education and
health, as well as for power, irrigation and roads sectors.



Annex A
Page 3 of 11
The Fiscal Reform & Public Sector Restructuring Program
6.    Wide-ranging reforms cannot succeed unless they are carried out in a completely transparent
manner, and have the backing of a strong public consensus. In 1998, we published a White Paper on the
State's Finances which brought to public attention the state's deteriorating fiscal position. Since then, we
have published several sectoral policy papers, including one on power and another on irrigation sector
reforms. The Fiscal Reform and Public Sector Restructuring Program is spelt out in five Policy
Papers - i.e., Medium Term Fiscal Reform Policy, Reforms in Governance, Civil Service Reforms, Public
Enterprise Reform and Privatization and Financial Management & Accountability Strategy. These will be
tabled, after Cabinet Approval, in the State Legislative Assembly for public debate on March 27, 2000.
The paragraphs below summarize the major provisions of these Papers, as does the attached Matrix of
Medium-Term Benchmarks/Actions (The Matrix).
Medium-term Fiscal Policy
7.    The main objectives of our medium-term fiscal policy include: (a) reducing the fiscal deficit so
that the ratio of debt to Gross State Domestic Product (GSDP) and interest payments to revenues begin to
decline; (b) improving the composition of public spending to enhance its developmental impact; and (c)
reforming the tax system to improve efficiency in resource allocation. The extent of required fiscal
deficit reduction over the next five years (1999-2004) is considerable.
8.   In order to stabilize the level of its debt and debt service ratios, the Government is committed to
eliminate the primary deficit (the gap between revenue and non-interest expenditure), estimated at about
3.5 percent in 1998-99, and turn it into a surplus by 2003-2004. This is inclusive of the projected
budgetary support for financial restructuring of the power sector and Voluntary Retirement Scheme
(VRS) of workers in public enterprises that will be closed/divested. The overall fiscal deficit is targeted
to decline from 7.5 percent of GSDP in 1998-99 to about 4 percent by 2003-2004. Although power sector
reform will result in very large benefits for the Government's budgetary condition over the medium term,
a substantial part of the fiscal deficit over the next few years is attributable to the costs of restructuring the
power sector and preparing for privatization. The deficit net of power sector requirements is targeted to
decline from 6.7 percent to less than 3 percent. Over this period, the state's own tax revenues is projected
to rise from 5.4 percent to 7.6 percent and public investment together with non-wage O&M to rise from
less than 2 percent of GSDP to about 4 percent. Social sector spending, including through devolution to
local bodies, is anticipated to rise from 3 percent of GSDP in 1998-99 to 4 percent by 2003-2004.
9.    There has already been measurable improvement in the state's fiscal management over the past
twelve months. The fiscal deficit is expected to decline from 7.5 percent of GSDP in 1998-1999 to 7.0
percent in 1999-2000. Consistent with the medium-term fiscal framework, the Government aims at a
further reduction of the fiscal deficit to no more than 6.0 percent of GSDP in 2000-2001. Net of the
power sector's projected financial restructuring requirements, the deficit is targeted to decline from 6.0
percent of GSDP in 1999-2000 to 4.5 percent in 2000-2001. To help us achieve our fiscal targets,
contingency plans are being prepared on an annual basis, starting in 1999-2000, to enable mid-course
revisions.
10.   The Government has imposed strict controls over its debt and guarantees. Given the projected
increases in our sectoral investments, the debt stock to GSDP ratio is expected to continue to rise initially,
though more slowly than in the recent past, to peak at about 42.0 percent in 2002-2003 and then begin to
decline. We have also decided to better regulate growth of state guarantees by requiring an escrow
arrangement; and we are considering various options for capping guarantees, proposed by the Reserve
Bank of India, and will adopt an appropriate one in 2000-2001. Outstanding guarantees will remain in the
range of 2.5-3 percent of GSDP over the medium term.
Public Expenditure Policy and Management
11.   Our strategy for improving public expenditure policy and management includes the following
measures.



Annex A
Page 4 of 11
12.   First, to stabilize debt, we are targeting a cut in overall non-interest expenditure, from 16.6 percent
in 19999-2000 to 15.3 percent in 2003-2004.
13.   Second, growth in the wage bill is being controlled by restrictions on recruitment already in place
so as to downsize the civil service by about 2 percent annually (see further under Civil Service Reforms).
We have also taken additional austerity measures such as abolishing staff entitlement to encash
outstanding leave.
14.   Third, towards reducing the burden of non-merit subsidies, we have raised the rates of user charges
for professional education and hospital care by 50-100 percent and decided to raise canal irrigation user
charges in 2000-2001. Explicit subsidies non-merit are targeted to fall from 1.4 percent of GSDP 1998-
1999 to 0.7 percent in 2003-2004.
15.   Fourth, we have identified a category of "high priority developmental expenditures", which
includes non-wage O&M and capital investments in irrigation, roads, education and health sectors as well
as the up-front costs of reforms in the power sector and other public enterprises. We have committed to
protect this category of expenditures from any budget cuts and to enhance significantly budgetary
expenditure on this category in the coming years. Orders have been issued to all treasuries to ensure that
high-priority development expenditures get priority in clearance of bills. We have also requested the
Comptroller and Auditor General of India for a special audit of these components of the expenditure
budget, to be conducted at the end of each fiscal year.
16.   Fifth, we have prepared a strategy for establishing a Medium-Term Expenditure Framework
(MTEF). The MTEF will begin to be implemented in 2000-2001 and be fully in place the following year.
17.   Sixth, we have decided to conduct annual public expenditure reviews and strengthen, over time,
capacity in the government to evaluate the effectiveness of public spending.
18.   Seventh, we have decided to review our current pension and provident fund systems. A study of
reform options will be carried out in 2000-2001, with reforms themselves to be initiated in 2001-2002.
19.   Eighth, and finally, a number of measures have been taken to improve Expenditure ControL The
practice of depositing unspent balances at the end of the year into the Personal Ledger Accounts has been
terminated. A new system of reporting time and cost over runs and stricter requirements for new project
starts have been established. Related measures to improve financial management are discussed below
(see Financial Management and Accountability).
Tax Reforns
20.   The State's goals in tax reform are to substantially enhance its own tax revenue and create a fairer
and simpler tax system. The Government's reform strategy envisages replacement of our main tax, the
sales (or trade) tax, by a VAT in 2004, after adequate preparation and in coordination with other states
and the government of India. Over the next four years, the strategy focuses on improving both tax
policies and tax administration.
21.   The Government has abolished the costly and unproductive sales tax incentives on new investment
in the state, including both tax holidays and deferral schemes. As a first step towards rationalisation of
sales tax rates, the Government has implemented the Floor Rates in accordance with the agreement
reached among all states and the Centre. Further steps to reduce the number of rates from 11 to no more
than 5, and to reduce the commodity classification from about 1400 to about 200 will be implemented in
2000-01. An entry tax has been introduced on selected commodities including non-levy sugar and
tobacco, and its scope is proposed to further expand in 2000-01. The Motor Vehicle Tax Act has been
amended to enable the government to make annual revisions in rates through executive orders.
22.   Estimates suggest that our campaign to curb evasion of sales tax has yielded at least Rs.200 crore
additional revenue during 1999-2000. The Cabinet has approved amendments to the Trade Tax Act,
which governs the sales tax, to strengthen enforcement and curb evasion practices such as under-valuation



Annex A
Page 5 of 11
and non-registration. A pilot survey of unregistered dealers in 2 districts has revealed that 15 percent of
the surveyed dealers are required to register. Steps have been initiated to modernise tax administration
through the introduction of computerized procedures. A complete set of customised software has been
developed and tested for acceptance. Over the next 2 years, a functional restructuring of the Trade Tax
Department will be completed, which will place greater emphasis on better staff recruitment and training
and a departmental anti-corruption strategy. By 2003-2004, the state would rely on self-assessment, with
appropriate audit procedures, and the introduction of modem information technology statewide to reduce
direct interface with tax payers. The system of land registration and property valuation will also be
modernized, leading to increased yield from stamp duties and the urban property tax.
Civil Service Reforms (CSR)
23.   The Government's strategy to combat issues of low productivity, overstaffing, under-utilization
and limited skills , as outlined in our Civil Service Reform Paper, has three components. First, to
strengthen our fiscal position, we are targeting a global downsizing of at least 2 percent per year from
1998-1999 to 2003-2004. This will yield an estimated savings of about Rs.20 billion, or 1 percentage
point of GSDP. The 2 percent downsizing target has been chosen on the basis of an annual attrition rate
of 3 percent, to allow for increased staffing in critical areas such as rural health care education and law
enforcement. We recognize that much greater downsizing and redeployment is likely to be necessary in
certain areas, such as Irrigation and Public Works. Already about 10,000 vacant positions identified as
redundant in various departments have been abolished.
24.   The second thrust of the reforms is to enhance the productivity of the civil service. This will be
initiated via a series of inter and intra-departmental functional reviews. The first such reviews will be
conducted during 2000-2001 for five key departments that are considered the most important for
improving economic performance and reducing poverty. Four of these departments, Forestry, Irrigation,
Medicine and Health, and Public Works, comprise over 30 percent of all civil servants in UP. The fifth,
the Trade Tax Department, plays a critical role for the state's own tax mobilization effort. GoUP's
detailed plan for civil service reform in the departments is presented in the Policy Paper on Civil Service
Reforms.
25.   Third, we aim to improve meritocracy within the civil service and restore managerial authority,
through a variety of measures including: establishing a computerized human resource database,
improving existing systems for performance monitoring and evaluation, allocating more for training,
improving training processes, and reducing transfers. With regards to reducing transfers we have:
finalized and publicized guidelines for the appointment and transfer of senior and mnid-level officers and
are monitoring compliance with the same (the annual transfer policy has been placed on the web);
classified job requirements for all senior positions (the top 1,800 civil service positions within the state);
and established a consultative mechanism to oversee transfers, which has already been initiated in the
case of the most senior officers.
Anti-Corruption and Deregulation Strategy
26.   The key objective of the government's reform in this area, as elaborated in our Governance Reform
paper, is to reduce wastage of public resources, and increase efficiency, transparency and accountability
of all departments. Our strategy has four main components. First, we have commissioned a series of
independent surveys of officials, businesses and the public. The survey results will be fed into the
production of a detailed anticorruption strategy and published, along with this strategy, in the year 2000.
Follow-up surveys will be conducted at two-year intervals to monitor progress in the wake of the reforms.
27.   Second, we will pursue deregulation to reduce opportunities for corruption in the administration of
rules and regulations. Internal reviews will be conducted by Policy Planning Cells, which have been
established in 62 major departments, while the high-level Deregulation Commnittee we have established
will conduct an external review with the objective of streamlining the administrative and regulatory
framework.



Annex A
Page 6 of 11
28.   Third, we have already begun strengthening the key accountability institutions: Vigilance
Department and the Lok Ayukta, or Ombudsman. The Govermnent will significantly increase the
resource allocation to both the Vigilance Establishment and the Lok Ayukta in 2000-2001, and these
institutions will be given more control over staff-hiring. Vacancies in the Lok Ayukta organization will be
filled and the backlog of Ayukta reports - stretching back to 1991 - will be submitted during 2000-2001
to the Legislative Assembly. Further institutional strengthening is expected in 2000-2001 from the
recommendations of a Governance Reform  Task Force we have established.  The task force
recommendations will be implemented in 2001-2002.
29.   Fourth, to make government more responsive to the citizens, we have published citizens charters
for 18 Government Departments specifying service standards. The Cabinet has approved a code of access
for documents of local government, state financial institutions and development agencies. A Freedom of
Information Act, covering a more comprehensive access, will be submitted to the Legislative Assembly
for consideration in 2000-2001.
Decentralization to Rural Local Bodies
30.   Beginning with the 1999-00 Budget in March 1999, the Government has initiated a far reaching
program of decentralization and devolution of powers and responsibilities to elected local bodies in the
countryside, called Panchayati Raj Institutions (PRIs). Key measures introduced to date include the
following: (i) transfer of responsibility for providing village level services to lowest tier of rural local
bodies - including elementary education, reproductive health, drinking water supply, etc.; (ii) transfer of
corresponding assets; (iii) transfer of control of corresponding staff (65,000 regular staff; 275,000
teachers) ; (iv) redefinition of accountability relations between the executive and elected local
government bodies at the district level; (v) empowerment of local bodies to recruit new staff at terms and
conditions to be determined by them; (vi) transfer of funds to the local bodies according to the formula
recommended by the State Finance Commission (4 percent of the state's own net tax revenue to be
devolved to the rural local bodies); (vii) constitution of village level committees to ensure collective
decision making; and (viii) preparation of a comprehensive training manual for all local body
functionaries.
31.   Based on experience with implementation of measures undertaken to date, the Government plans
to continue to decentralize with the objectives of improving the quality of social services and
infrastructure maintenance in rural areas, and increasing the accountability of government.
Public Enterprise Reform
32.   The main objective of the government's Public Enterprise (PE) reform program is to increase
efficiency in the use of public resources and reduce the managerial and financial burden on the
government. The successful implementation of the program would result in: a reduction in fiscal drain
caused by continued subsidization and non-performing loans to loss making enterprises; release of assets
and resources to private owners who are likely to manage them more productively; and reorientation of
the government's role away from commercial activities.
33. The Govemment's Policy Paper on Public Enterprise Reform and Privatization details the
Government's policy of divestment, privatization or closure of enterprises which compete with the private
sector or are fundamentally commercial in nature. The Paper provides guidelines for an open and
transparent divestiture or closure of the public enterprises including detailed procedures for
divestment/closure, voluntary retirement and for dealing with environmental issues, which have been
approved by the Cabinet. The Policy Paper also includes procedures for implementation of the First
Phase of the program by March 2001, involving 20 industrial units and about 20,000 workers. A
Divestment Commnission has been established and staffed. One enterprise, Uttar Pradesh Instrumentation
Limited, has been closed and its assets sold this year and progress is being made in other units.
34.  In each of the two years after 2000-2001, about eight industrial units of PEs are proposed to be
divested or closed in accordance with the communications of the Divestment Commission. By March



Annex A
Page 7 of 11
2004, the PE reform program is expected to be completed with divestiture or closure of those PE's which
meet the policy's basic criteria. Only a few enterprises that serve important social aims are expected to
still be in government ownership by March 2004.
Financial Management & Accountability
35.   Our Financial Management and Accountability Strategy Paper puts forward three main strategies
to strengthen mechanisms for financial management and accountability.
36.   First, to strengthen financial control, we plan to modernize and computerize the financial
management and control architecture, including internal controls, accounting and management
information systems across the state. We have created and filled a new position of a Financial
"Controller" to provide leadership in modernizing the system of financial management.  Having
computerized the transactions in 90 treasury offices, we plan to enhance the state's treasury function by
converting the treasury offices into Integrated Pay and Accounts Offices next year. Also as of next year,
major departments will be required to publish revised annual reports outlining clearly their objectives,
activities, costs and actual achievement.
37.   Second, to strengthen audit, we will in 2000-01 create the position of "State Internal Auditor". We
will consolidate the internal audit function and modernize internal audit practice. We are already ensuring
timely response by the government to the audit reports by Controller and Auditor General (CAG).
38.   Third, we propose to organize a national conference on "Parliamentary Control of the Public
Purse" in 2000 to build national consensus in favour of increased legislative scrutiny. To improve public
scrutiny, we have established a task force, with members from outside as well as within government, to
recommend modes of presentation and frequency of government financial statements to the public. Action
on the recommendations of the Task Force will be taken next year. The Public Accounts Comrnittee of
the States Legislature has been reactivated, which will further strengthen the scrutiny of public
expenditure.
39.   Fourth, and finally, we have arranged with C &AG to conduct audits of high priority expenditures
and of procurement undertaken by major departments.
Reform Implementation and Monitoring
40.   A number of bodies are being created to implement the above reforms. Governance reforms will
be overseen by the Governance and CSR Core Group, headed by the Chief Secretary. Fiscal and financial
management reforms will be led by the Reform Implementation Task Force, headed by the Principal
Secretary of Finance. For tax reforms, a Reform Working Group will be formed in the Trade Tax
Department early in 2000-2001. A Divestment Commission is leading the PE reform program.
41.   To assist with reform implementation, a number of technical assistance assignments will be
needed. These cover the areas of: public enterprise reform, tax reform, implementation of property tax
reforms, civil service reform, anti-corruption measures, public expenditure management, financial
management and poverty monitoring. Funding from the World Bank has been requested for these.
42.   Monitoring of the Reforn  Program. The Government has prepared a list of Reform
Process/Outcome Indicators on each component of the reform program. These indicators will be
monitored on a regular basis during the period of implementation of reforms. In addition, these
indicators, together with the Poverty and Social Monitoring System (PSMS) indicators (see below), will
be tracked by a Monitoring Unit that has been set up in the office of the Chief Secretary for this purpose.
Poverty and Social Monitoring System
43.   Our ultimate objective in undertaking reform is to reduce poverty. An effective monitoring system
will enable the Government to not only to mitigate any negative impacts of reform, but also to better link
policies and expenditures to human development outcomes. A Poverty and Social Monitoring System
(PSMS) has been set up in the Office of the Chief Secretary. The system's main components are:



Annex A
Page 8 of 11
identification of a set of performance indicators and targets to track progress in reducing poverty and
promoting human development; building capacity to better use the extensive statistical information that is
already being collected as well as collect new information; and preparing analysis of the information to
understand the impact of the reforms on the poor and inform policy decisions.
Need for Extraordinary Financing
44.   Reforms have costs to begin with, while the fiscal gains take time to materialise. Restructuring
the power sector, for instance, has upfront financial costs so as to begin afresh, whereas there are
significant gains over the medium-term as the budgetary burden will vanish. Recovery of costs of public
services through user charges requires that the quality of service delivery first shows some improvement,
which requires additional allocations for operation and maintenance.  Closures, divestment and
privatisation of public enterprises will entail up-front costs including the cost of separation benefits. In
addition to these direct costs, there is the risk of expenditure cuts affecting non-wage O&M and capital
outlays in key sectors. Hence the protection of high-priority spending also needs to be factored into the
costs of reforms.
45.   Much of these costs of reforms will be met by controlling expenditure and augmenting revenue.
Nevertheless, extraordinary financing including quick disbursing external assistance will be required in
the initial years of the reform programn. Preliminary projections indicate that the extraordinary financing
requirement will decline over the years - from Rs 1800 crore about (US$425 million) in 1999-2000 to
about Rs. 1300 crore in 2000-2001 and Rs.700 crore in 2001-2002.
Commitment and Request
46.   The Government of Uttar Pradesh is committed to implement the reform Program as set out in the
five above-mentioned Policy Papers, and as summnarized in this letter and in the attached Matrix of
Medium-term Actions. We have already completed the first phase of reforms, as detailed in the first
column of the Matrix. We are determined to complete the Program, and thereby to accelerate economic
and social development in the state of Uttar Pradesh.
47.   I hereby request that this Letter of Development Policy be forwarded to the President of the World
Bank for consideration of financial assistance in support of our reform Program.
With regards,
Dr. Yogendra Narain
Chief Secretary
Government of Uttar Pradesh

POLICY PAPER
ON
UTTAR PRADESH
MEDIUM TERM FISCAL
REFORM POLICY
Government of Uttar Pradesh






Annex B
Final Copy
Mlarch 17, 2000
Page 2 of 19
Uttar Pradesh
Medium Term Fiscal Reform Policy
1.    Preamble
The Government of Uttar Pradesh has formulated its fiscal reform policy
as one of the main pillars of its comprehensive medium-term strategy to stabilize
the financial position of the state and reverse the trend of declining economic
performance and persistence of poverty. The comprehensive strategy includes
governance and civil service reform and power sector reform, public enterprise
reform, financial management reform, as well as reforms in policies and
institutions pertaining to other key infrastructure (irrigation and roads) and social
(education and health) sectors. The aim of the comprehensive reform strategy is
to achieve fiscal sustainability and create an enabling environment for improved
economic performance and more rapid poverty reduction, so that over the
medium-term, Uttar Pradesh can reach the top half among Indian states in terms
of physical, social and human development indicators.
The populous and sprawling State of Uttar Pradesh is well endowed with
rich natural resources in the form of vast fertile lands, plentiful surface and
ground water resources, forest and livestock resources. In spite of its rich natural
resources and vast manpower, the State of Uttar Pradesh continues to be among
the most backward in the country in terms of socio-economic indicators.
Uttar Pradesh has lagged behind the national average in the growth
process considerably except for a brief period of 1974-79 and 1990-92. As a
consequence, the distance between the State and national per capita income
has been constantly widening. In 1996-97, U.P's per capita income is currently
less than two third of the national per capita income. The relative gap has
tended to widen at an accelerated pace during the nineties when national growth
rate accelerated, while State growth rate remains only marginally above the
population growth rate.
Table I: Differential Growth Rates of UP and Indian Economy
(Percentage Annual)

The percentage of people below the poverty line in U.P. was 40.9%
against the all-India figure of 35.9% in 1993-94. Though this percentage has
gone down over the years, yet the absolute number of persons living below the
poverty line has increased from 530.60 lakhs (53 million) in 1983-84 to 604.40
lakhs in 1993-94. It is clear that poverty cannot be reduced without reversing the
trend of declining rates of economic growth.
U.P. lags behind most of the major States in terms of the level of
infrastructure facilities. With respect to electricity, roads and irrigation, U.P. is far
behind the faster growing States like Punjab, Haryana, Gujarat, Maharashtra
and Karnataka (Annexure I). Not only is the economic infrastructure inadequate
but it is also of poor quality. Over the years, the relative gap in infrastructure
between the developed States and U.P. has widened.
In terms of social indicators too, the situation is far from satisfactory, with
U.P. at the bottom among Indian States. The fertility level is the highest in India
at 4.8, affecting both population growth and the health of women and children.
The infant mortality rate is 85 per .1,000 compared to 72 for India as a whole.
(Annexure II). The demographic situation presents a worrisome picture.
II.   Reasons for poor economic performance
The general economic backwardness and the slowing down of the
development process can be attributed to many factors, chief among which are:
(I) Inadequate and poor quality of infrastructure, especially power supply
shortages, reflecting the low level of investment in the economy over the years,
both in the public as well as private sectors, (ii) inadequate and ineffective
spending on education and health resulting in a low level of human resource
development and (iii) frequent changes in the government, resulting in a
perceptible decline in the quality of governance. In fact, all these factors are inter
related.



Annex B
Final Copy
March 17, 2000
Page 4 of 19
The failure of the State Government to maintain the desired level of public
investment in infrastructure and social services can be traced to the deteriorating
situation of the State finances since the early eighties and to the increased
political instability in the 1992-97 period. The mounting revenue expenditure of
the State Government on staff salaries, pension, interest payment and subsidies
have forced a squeeze in its capital and maintenance expenditures. The capital
expenditure which was around one third of total expenditure in the early eighties
has come down to below one fifth in the nineties. Similarly the ratio of plan
expenditure to total expenditure has also sharply come down from around two
fifths to one fifth, reflecting the decline in expenditure flexibility. The composition
of plan expenditure itself has shifted in favour of current expenditure at the cost
of capital expenditure. The efficiency of public spending has declined.
The slowing down of public investment has had its impact on the pace of
private investment in the State. The declining credit-deposit ratio and the share of
loans and advances by the national financial institutions is one reflection of the
showing down of economic activity in the State.
The low level of human resource development reflects substantial under-
investment in education and health services in the State. This causes great
deprivation to large masses of people. It restricts their capabilities to benefit from
the limited opportunities available to them, on the one hand, and lowers the
growth potential of the state economy due to low productivity levels, on the other.
U.P. has been caught in a vicious circle. The fiscal, governance and
infrastructure problems have fed into each other. Slow economic growth has
made increasing the revenue yield difficult. Poor governance has further lowered
the revenue performance. Financially weak infrastructure sectors have placed a
massive burden on the state exchequer. The fiscal stress caused by the setting
of tariffs well below cost-recovery levels has been added to by the deepening
culture of non-payment of customers to public utilities. The fiscal crisis has
prevented the state from investing enough to provide the foundation for growth
and lift social indicators to acceptable levels.
Ill.  Fiscal Crisis
U.P is facing an extremely adverse fiscal situation. From a revenue
surplus of Rs. 250 crores in 1987-88, the State now has a large and rising
revenue deficit, which reached Rs. 8696 crores in 1998-99. Revenue Deficit as a
percentage of Revenue Expenditure, which represents the proportion of
operating costs that are financed by borrowed resources, reached 33% in 1998-
99. Instead of using revenue account surpluses to partially finance public
investments, as is the financially prudent course, the Government is making
future generations pay for current operating costs.



Annex B
Final Copy
March 17, 2000
Page S of 19
The adjusted' gross fiscal deficit reached Rs. 11341 crores or 7.5% of
estimated GSDP in 1998-99. As a result of high and continued deficits, running
for more than a decade, indebtedness of the State has risen sharply. The stock
of debt is as high as 35% of GSDP, while interest payments constituted 35% of
the total revenue receipts in 1998-99. Some of the other important dimensions of
the fiscal crisis are as follows:
(i)   Revenue (recurring) expenditure has reached over 150%  of revenue
receipts2 in the State.
(ii)  Interest payment as percentage of total revenue expenditure has gone
from 14% in 1986-87 to 19% in 1998-99.
(iii)  The average tax rate in UP is only 50% of the level in the middle income
States. Since tax compliance in the State is relatively poor, the actual tax
revenue realised is almost one-fourth in comparison to these States.
(iv)   Expenditure on salary and allowances and pension of regular government
servants is estimated to have reached 40% of the State's total revenue in
1999-00; along with interest payments, they would amount to 75% of
revenue.
(v)   Capital expenditure and net lending has come down from 22% of total
expenditure in 1987-88 to 7% in 1997-98.
(vi)  The ratio of development & non-development expenditure in total revenue
expenditure has come down from 67: 33 in 1986-87 to 53: 44 in 1998-99.
(vii)  While the salary of class IlIl and class IV employees has increased by
nearly 75 and over 100 times, respectively, between 1964 and 1998, user
charges have actually declined in some areas.
(viii)  In the 50 Public Sector Units, the total investment of State Government
was to the tune of Rs. 13127 crores till January 1997 but the returns
received against these investments were negligible.
IV.  Medium-Term Fiscal Framework
Recognizing the unsustainability of prevailing trends, the State
Government has decided to launch a medium-term fiscal reform strategy to
achieve the following outcomes : (a) stabilization of the debt to GSDP and debt
servicing to revenue ratios, so that these ratios are on a declining trend by
2004-05, (b) improving the composition of public spending to enhance its
developmental impact and (c) reforming the tax system to improve efficiency in
resource allocation. In order to stabilize the level of its debt and debt service
ratios over the medium-term, the Government of U.P will need to eliminate the
primary deficit (the gap between revenue and non-interest expenditure) and turn
it into a surplus by 2003-04. The gross adjustment will have to be greater, to
enable the state to meet the upfront cost of reforms (including the cost of
See annex /il for explanation of adjustments to the fiscal deficit.
2     Refer to Annex 11l for the definitions of adjusted revenue receipts and revenue
expenditure.



Annex B
Final Copy
March 17, 2000
Page 6 of 19
restructuring power and other PEs) and increase its spending on infrastructure
and human resources, while at the same time reducing the deficit.
The Government's medium-term fiscal reform strategy targets to reduce
the fiscal deficit from 7.5% of GSDP in 1998-99 to about 4% by 2003-04 and to
3.3% by 2004-05. Over this period, the-primary deficit of 3.5% in 1998-99 is
targeted to turn into a surplus of about 1% of GSDP, while the state's own tax
revenues are projected to rise from 5.4% of GSDP to 7.9%.

In the absence of the reforms, the state's financial crisis would further
intensify leading to a collapse of development expenditures and a halt in most of
the government's operations and social service provisions The vicious circle
would continue and the per-capita income of the state would stagnate. Even
though the fiscal deficit is limited by the borrowing capacity of the state,
exhausting this borrowing capacity in conditions.of economic stagnation would
lead to an explosive growth path of the debt/GSDP ratio ; this ratio is projected to



Annex B
Final Copy
March 17, 2000
Page 7 of 19
rise under the No Reform Scenario from 34% in March 1999 to 51% by March
2005.
Under the Reform Scenario envisaged by the Government of U.P, the
additional public investments in infrastructure and human capital, improvements
in governance and the policy and institutional climate, and reforms in critical
sectors including power, are together expected to "crowd in" private investment
and gradually raise the rate of economic growth as the reforms take hold over the
medium term, thereby beginning to reverse the trend of widening gap between
UP and the rest of India. The fiscal, governance and sector reforms will lead to
the creation of additional fiscal space and enable the State to sustain higher
levels of public investment and non-wage operation and maintenance (O&M) in
irrigation, roads and social services as well as to improve the quality of services
rendered.
The key effects of the proposed reforms on the State's fiscal situation
include: (I) a significant increase in resource mobilization through increases in
the state's own taxes and cost recovery for public services provided by the state;
(ii) substantial improvement in the composition of expenditures through increases
in spending on high priority developmental expenditures along with significant
reductions in unproductive expenditures such as subsidies (particularly through
reforms in power and irrigation sectors), wages and salaries (through civil service
reform) and those related to losses of public enterprises (through PE reform and
privatization). Under this scenario, the debt-to GSDP ratio and the debt service-
revenue ratio would both begin to decline over the medium-term. The
government would regulate and control the issuing of guarantees through the
levy of guarantee fee, requirement of escrow arrangement (set aside fund) and
adopting a ceiling on outstanding guarantees, after studying the various options
proposed by RBI. Outstanding guarantees will remain below 3% of GSDP over
the medium-term, excluding those that may be issued to support the financial
restructuring of the power sector.
The medium-term fiscal framework of the reform scenario is shown in
Annexure Ill. The projections are based on assuming a 6% rate of annual
inflation and 11% interest rate on new loans in future years. The reforms and
improved targeting and quality of public spending, along with the induced higher
level of private investment, are projected to raise the rate of economic growth
from the current level of about 3%-3.5% to around 5% by 2004-05.
The Government of Uttar Pradesh expects, as a result of the
implementation of the medium-term fiscal reform policies, the Fiscal Deficit of the
State to decline not only in proportion to State income, but also in absolute rupee
terms. Adding principal repayment obligations, the gross financing requirement
will remain in the range of around Rs.12000-12500 crores in each of the next 5
years. The plan is to reduce over the medium-term the share of financing from
the most expensive sources, such as small savings loans and market borrowing,
while enhancing the share of project financing by external donors. In the initial



Annex B
Final Copy
March 17, 2000
Page 8 of 19
years of the reform program, the State would need extraordinary financing, from
the Government of India and from external donors, to enable financing of the up-
front costs of reforms, including PE reform and Power Sector restructuring, and
to adequately protect High Priority Development Experditures. Such
extraordinary financing is projected to be phased out in the later years, as the
reforms yield additional revenue and expenditure savings.
V.   Revenue Enhancement Strategy
The State's own revenue to GSDP ratio has been hovering around 5%-6%
over the last several years. The Government is determined to raise this ratio to
over 8% by 2002-03 and close to 9% by 2004-05, through reforming the tax
system, introduction of new taxes and strengthening the cost recovery efforts in
the irrigation, power, transport, education and health sectors.
Trade Tax Reform: Evasion of the state's first point sales tax, called
the UP Trade Tax, the state's biggest tax instrument, is estimated to be as high
as 40% of the revenues being collected. The Trade Tax Department (TTD) has
identified the main forms of evasion and initiated short-term measures to combat
each form of evasion. This short-term strategy is focused on reducing tax
evasion through stricter enforcement of the law and effective use of information
collected at the state's border check posts. At the same time, the Government
has recognised the need to develop a medium-term strategy for reforming and
modernising the sales tax policy and administration. The medium term goal of
trade tax reform is a more simple and modern tax system which will lead to
improved voluntary compliance, reduced opportunities for lengthy legal disputes
or taxpayer harassment, and improved climate for private sector growth. The
medium-term strategy would consist of the following sequence of steps:
*    Implementation of floor rates to rationalise the Trade Tax, in
accordance with agreement among all States (1999-00);
*    Rationalisation of tax incentives to ban any new tax holidays or
deferral (1999-00);
*    Amendment of the Trade Tax Act to strengthen the anti-evasion
drive by curbing under-valuation of goods, non-registration of
dealers and to prevent unnecessary delays and curb frivolous
appeals (2000-01);
*    Reduction in the number of Trade Tax rates from 11 to 5, in line
with Phase I of the recommendations of the government
commissioned Report by the Institute for Social and Economic
Change, Bangalore (2000-01);
*    Computerisation of existing business processes on pilot basis
(2000-01);
*    Reorganisation of the TTD based on functional design (begin in
2000-01 and to be completed in 2001-02);
*    Improved staff training and recruitment (starting 2000-01);



Annex B
Final Copy
March 17, 2000
Page 9 of 19
*    Implement external audit of the Trade Tax Department on a sample
basis by an external professional auditing firm (2000-01);
*    Make officials of the Trade Tax Department subject to Vigilance
supervision and require all tax officers to make an annual
declaration of their family assets (2000-01);
*    Further rationalisaton  of Trade  Tax  rates and  commodity
classification, in line with Phase II of the recommendations of the
government commissioned Report by the Institute for Social and
Economic Change, Bangalore (2001-02 Budget);
*    Scaling up of computerization (2001-02);
*    Business process re-engineering (study in 2001-02 and implement
in 2002-03);
*    Introduction of Self-Assessment and modern IT-based Audit
procedures (start in 2002-03 and complete in 2003-04);
*    Amendment of Trade Tax Act in preparation for VAT (2003-04); and
*    Introduction of VAT (2004);
Motor Vehicle Tax Reforms: In November 1998 the Government of U.P
brought into force a number of reforms. A presumptive design was adopted for
buses to minimise opportunities for evasion. The Act was revised to convert the
lifetime levy on personal four-wheel vehicles from specific to ad valorem rates.
The State plans to
l    Further revise the rates applicable to the presumptive base of the
passenger bus tax, which is currently defined in terms of seats and
hence lacks buoyancy, by at least 10% (1 999-00);
o    Amend the Act again to enable the rates to be revised annually
through Government Orders (1999-00); and
O-I   Revise rates annually, starting in 2001-02.
Stamp Duty and Registration: 'n order to create a more transparent
system for the determination of stamp duty liability, to ensure fair administration
of stamp duties and registration fees, and to adequately conserve property
conveyance records for the future, the State plans to
El    Modernise property valuation determination using standardised
objective  characteristics  and   computerised   administrative
procedures (starting 2000-01);
oI   Introduce appropriate scanning technology for the storage of
registered conveyance deeds in two Tehsil registry offices, on a
pilot basis (2000-01); and
O    Expand the scanning and storage of stamp duty and land
registration system state-wide (2001-02).
New Entry Tax: An Entry Tax, which is in effect an additional sales tax on
selected commodities including those on which a sales tax cannot be levied by
the state, has been introduced through a Government Order issued on
November 1, 1999. Phased implementation has begun in December 1999.



Annex B
Final Copy
March 17, 2000
Page 10 of 19
User Charges: Towards improving the rate of cost recovery from public
provision of private and semi-private goods and services, the Government will
implement a program of phased increase in user charges pertaining to power,
irrigation, higher education, specialised hospital services and other selected
economic and social services. The medium-term strategy would consist of the
following steps:
C    Upward adjustment of power tariffs as recommended by the
Electricity Regulatory Committee
0]    Upward adjustment of canal irrigation rates to double the rate of
cost recovery from 9% to 18% (1999-00, effective from April 1,
2000);
0     Revision of fees in engineering, medical, other colleges and
secondary schools to double the rate of cost recovery from 6% to
12% (1999-00, effective from April 1, 2000);
0    Double cost -recovery  from  hospital and veterinary services
(1999-00);
0     Implement second  round  of rate revisions in all of the above
(2001-02, effective from April 1, 2002)
VI.  Expenditure Reprioritization Strategy
Over the medium term, government spending will need to be increased in
both absolute and relative terms on elementary education, primary and
secondary health care, high priority investment and maintenance including
irrigation system rehabilitation and strengthening of roads. This will be possible
only if government spending is significantly reduced on subsidies, on wages and
salaries (through civil service form) and on budget support to loss making
enterprises.
The strategy to reprioritize expenditure will have the following key
elements:
(a)   The total number of government employees/officers would be
brought down by at least 2% every year for the next 5 years, to
achieve a reduction of at least 1 percentage point of GSDP in the
salary bill;
(b)   Comprehensive plan to reduce non-merit subsidies and transfers -
including those for higher education, professional education, urban
water supply and urban hospitals -to bring them to the bare
minimum level of no more than 0.5% of GSDP by 2004-05;
(c)   Reform  of  pension  and  provident  fund  based  on  the
recommendations of a study to be commissioned in 2000-01.



Annex B
Final Copy
March 17, 2000
Page 11 of 19
VIl. Expenditure Management Reforms
Expenditure Management Reforms, along with Financial Management
Reforms (explained in the Governance Reform Policy), will improve and
modernise the processes of planning and budgeting, accounting and audit,
monitoring and evaluation of program performance and outcomes.
As a result of recent initiatives such as the computerisation of treasury
transactions, the Government of U.P has already begun to strengthen the
efficiency of cash management and to prioritize the release of scarce funds so as
to protect the high priority developmental items in the expenditure budget from
resource shortage. Over the next two years, a suitable Medium Term
Expenditure Framework (MTEF) will be designed and developed to ensure (i) a
realistic resource framework for budgeting; (ii) linking resource allocation to
sector priorities and policies, consistent with the medium-term fiscal framework;
(iii) financial sustainability and timely economic and social returns from ongoing
projects, (iv) adequate allocation and attention to operation and maintenance of
existing assets and (v) ensuring that borrowed resources are used only for
financially viable investments that yield returns adequate to service the debt
incurred.
The medium-term strategy of expenditure management reform would
consist of the following sequence of steps:
0    Begin  implementation  of strategy  to  protect high-priority
developmental expenditure (1999-00);
E]   Improve the timely availability of financial management information
through effective use of computerised treasuries (2000-01 );
0    Finalise and begin piloting a suitable MTEF for planning and
budgeting within a realistic resource envelope (2000-01);
1    Fully implement the MTEF in the context of preparing the budget for
2002-03 (during the year 2001-02);
0    Achieve  full  computerisation   of  budgeting,  expenditure
management and accounting by 2002-03.
VIII. Fiscal Correction in 1999-00
The Government of U.P has begun to implement the medium-term fiscal
reform strategy in the current year. The 1999-00 Budget emphasised the need
for financial discipline as a measure of development strategy. The Budget
speech recognised the need, on the one hand, for measures to augment financial
resources and productive investments, and on the other hand, measures to save
on unproductive expenditures so as to improve the composition and
effectiveness of public spending and at the same time reduce the deficit.



Annex B
Final Copy
March 17, 2000
Page 12 of 19
During the current year 1999-00, the Government of UP expects to reduce
the adjusted fiscal deficit from 7.5% of GSDP in 1998-99 to 7.0% in 1999-00.
This reduction is being achieved on the one hand by higher revenue collections
and on the other hand by compressing recurrent expenditures. As a
consequence, the ratio of revenue deficit to revenue expenditure, a measure of
the proportion of operating expenses financed by borrowed resources, is
expected to go down from 33% in 1998-99 to 26% in 1999-2000. The
Government has developed and is implementing a Contingency Plan to fulfill
these targets in the event of unforeseen revenue or financing shortfalls.
The State Government has already taken the following measures during
this current year 1999-00:
(i)   Revenue Measures
(a)   Entry tax has been levied on three commodities with effect from
1.11.99. It is expected to fetch Rs.50 crores during the remaining
months of the financial year. Coverage will be extended to two
more commodities including tobacco;
(b)  Intensive drive has been launched to recover the arrears of
Government dues;
(c)   Surveys of establishments that remain outside the tax net have
been started by the Trade Tax Department;
(d)   Rules for house tax revision have been notified on 16.6.99;
(e)   Floor rates for the trade tax have been implemented, and a study
has been initiated for further rationalisation to reduce the number of
rates to no more than five;
(f)   The costly and unproductive trade tax incentives for new
investments have been abolished;
(g)   A committee has been constituted under Chief Secretary to
examine all proposals to increase cost recovery; and
(h)   A legal amendment has been enacted to enable the Government to
make annual revisions in motor vehicle tax rates through executive
orders.
(ii)   Expenditure Saving Measures
(a)   Abolition of leave encashment facilities for Class I and 11
employees;
(b)   Ban on foreign travel;
(c)   Ban on air-travel by certain categories of officers.
(d)   Freeze on selective basis the grants-in-aid to the secondary and
higher secondary schools, degree colleges and universities at the
1998-99 levels;
(e)   Ban on creation of new posts and non-filling of vacant posts.



Annex B
Final Copy
March 17, 2000
Page 13 of 19
(f)   7000 posts lying vacant for more than a year have already been
abolished and the task of abolishing another 8000 identified posts
is underway;
(g)   Ban on inducting new educational institutions on grants-in-aid list;
and
(h)   Government has initiated a study to quantify the amount of subsidy
being given for various schemes through the budget so as to
reduce explicit subsidies and phase out those that can no longer be
justified on economic or social grounds.
The above measures are expected to result in significant
expenditure saving. Downsizing the civil service by at least 2% through
attrition will result in saving of roughly Rs. 100 crores. The freeze on
secondary and higher education subsidy at the previous year's level will
result in savings of about Rs. 107 crores.
(iii)  Expenditure Management and Control
(i)   The practice of transferring unspent amounts at the end of the year
to Personal Ledger Accounts, thereby inflating the accounted
expenditure and weakening expenditure management, has been
terminated;
(ii)   Treasury transactions are being computerized, to improve the
government's information system and cash flow management
during the year (About 90 percent of the treasuries in the state have
already been computerized);
(iii)  Treasuries have been instructed to give priority, in clearing bills, to
(a) resource linked projects and programs including centrally
sponsored and externally aided schemes, (b) non-wage operation
and maintenance ; and (c) those state funded projects that are
close to completion (i.e. where over 75% of total project cost has
been spent);
(iv)  The call letter for 2000-2001  budget preparation  made it
compulsory for all the departments to review their ongoing schemes
with a view to close the unproductive schemes.
(v)   Introduced a new system of reporting of time and cost over runs
and stricter requirements for new project starts;
IX.  Further Fiscal Correction in 2000-01
Consistent with the medium-term fiscal framework, the budget for 2000-01
will aim at a further reduction in the fiscal deficit by 1 percent of GSDP, even
while enhancing the allocations for high priority development expenditures by at
least 0.5% of GSDP. This will be achieved through further containment of non-
developmental spending and increased tax yield, mainly through base



Annex B
Final Copy
March 17, 2000
Page 14 of 19
broadening and improved compliance. As in 1999-00, the Government will
prepare a Contingency Plan to compensate for unforeseen developments.
X.   Expected Outcome by 2004-05
As a result of continuing with the implementation of the envisaged multi-
year fiscal reform program during the period 2000-05, additional fiscal space will
be created, expenditure composition will improve and high priority additional
investments and social sector spending will be undertaken. At the same time, the
State's finances will be stabilised; both the debt-to-GSDP and debt service-to-
revenue ratios will be on a declining trend by 2004-05. A notable aspect of the
fiscal reforms, accompanied by governance reforms and sector reforms, will be
the high return on public sector spending in the areas of infrastructure and
human resources. There will be a "crowding in" impact whereby additional public
sector investments and improved service delivery will induce greater private
investment by increasing the productivity of the latter.
The fiscal reforms, accompanied by governance reforms (including
reforms in the civil service, public enterprises and financial management) and
sector reforms in power, irrigation, roads, health and education, will create an
enabling environment for improved economic performance and more rapid
poverty reduction. The implementation of this comprehensive reform program is
expected to take the State on to the path of recovery. It is expected that the rate
of growth of GSDP will reach around 5% by 2004-05 and the State will thereafter
be ready for a take off-stage in a serious attempt to reach the top half among
Indian states in terms of physical, social and human development indicators.
