Profile and Diagnostic of Poverty. A baseline study was undertaken to identify the key social and
economic factors responsible for high and sustained rates of poverty in Uttar Pradesh. The study draws
on a wide range of information - including (1) information obtained through official data sources (e.g. the
National Sample Surveys, administrative statistics from the Directorate of Economics and Statistics), (2) a
very recent survey of living conditions in poorer regions of the state that measured poverty across a range
of dimensions, and integrated conventional household and community survey methods with participatory
community-based approaches, and (3) a careful review of the existing literature in UP and India more
widely.
The work has given rise to a number of findings that have important ramifications for policy and program
design. Key findings are outlined briefly below.
First, the poor in UP comprise a heterogeneous group that can be broadly classifted into three
categories: the destitute, the structural poor, and the mobile poor. Each group faces different sets of
constraints and different options for economic and social mobility. Their needs are accordingly
different.
The poorest, best described as the destitute poor, are generally those that have experienced some
type of idiosyncratic shock that has permanently undermined their physical or hurnan resource
base. These shocks include events such as death or disability of a breadwinner, long-term and
costly illness of family members, desertion, alcoholism, mental illness, etc. Abandoned and
widowed women, particularly those with small children were prominently represented in this
category. Even dowry expenses can be a form of economic shock, in that households burdened by
dowry expenses for several daughters are said to be in danger of becoming seriously
impoverished. The destitute poor usually have no income source other than low-wage,
intermittently available casual labor or, in the worst case, foraging and begging. Their outlook
for the future is bleak.
A second group of poor households comprise the structural poor. These households tend to be
drawn from scheduled caste or tribal groups, suffer social discrimination and often live in isolated
hamlets far from public resources such as clean wells, irrigation sources, etc. As a general rule,
there is less mobility (either upward or downward) among this group than among other types of
poor households. They have inherited a stigmatized social identity that places them at a
disadvantage in interactions with higher castes, that is life-long, and that will be inherited in turn



Annex I
February 18,2000
Page 3 of 10
by their own children. However, there are encouraging signs that social identity is becoming less
of a factor in determining upward mobility for the urban poor. However the structural poor in
rural areas, like the destitute poor, are usually landless, dependent on casual labor, and tend to
acquire long-term debts which they are unable to discharge. Many look outside the traditional
village economy for opportunities for social and economic advancement.
The third group can be referred to as the mobile poor in that they demonstrate the strongest
potential for moving out of poverty. They are free from chronic debt, and often own some small
assets, usually land or livestock, that can be nurtured and developed into a productive resource. In
contrast to other types of poor households, the mobile poor view education as a very critical
factor in moving out of poverty - particularly those living in urban areas. Households which are
able to produce enough food, either through agriculture or employment, to feed all family
members throughout the twelve month of the year are perceived by the poor themselves to be the
only ones with real potential for upward mobility. These families, unless derailed by a sudden
shock, can avoid the trap of falling into debt in order to meet shortfalls in basic consumption and
possibly escape poverty by dint of unstinted effort. Many of these households are members of
social groups that are slightly higher and less stigmatized than the structural poor.
These categories are important because the individuals within often face different sets of
opportunities and constraints than individuals across groups. For example, the structural poor
may be constrained within the village economy in terms of the employment opportunities
available to them, and constrained in access to social networks that help them seek employment
outside the village. Facing a narrower set of employment opportunities may in turn influence
schooling decisions; the structural poor may perceive lower returns to investments in education.
One of the most important defining characteristic of the poor in UP is low levels of incomes, combined
with low asset endowments. They lack land, they lack education, they lack skills, and they lack the
kinds of social networks that help them find betterjobs and raise incomes. Further, they lack the
means and incentives to send their children to school or to invest in productive assets, perpetuating a
vicious cycle of poverty.
The overwhelming majority of poor people in UP work in low-skill, low-paid jobs with no
permanent tenure. Many are dependent on agriculture labor and work on lands owned by others.
Women often engage in the lowest paid activities - casual labor in fields, gathering firewood and
dung to sell in nearby towns, piece-work and construction activities in urban areas. Child labor is
common among the poorest and among socially disadvantaged households. Many of the poor
aspire to jobs outside the agriculture sector and income diversification has an important role in
economic mobility. However, employment opportunities in the non-farm sector are limited, and
the fortunate few who find jobs typically work for very low wages in services or small-scale
industries, again with no permanent tenure. Low-skill jobs in the rural non-farm sector often pay
no more than causal labor in agriculture. Many of the rural poor, particularly those from socially-
disadvantaged groups, lack the contacts and social networks necessary to secure employment
outside the local economy. And infrastructure constrains mobility and discourages the private
investment that leads to creation of jobs. Some of the poor move to the cities to seek
employment, where, if they are fortunate, they may eke out a living as casual laborers or in petty
service activities. Few have the skills or education necessary to flourish in the urban labor
market.
Education has a critically important role in economic mobility. In many regions of Uttar Pradesh,
lack of opportunity combined with limited assets creates a vicious cycle of poverty and illiteracy.



Annex I
February 18,2000
Page 4 of 10
The poorest are frequently unable to take advantage of the few opportunities that do exist because
they lack the knowledge, skills, and in many cases even basic literacy and nunmeracy. Education
becomes both a cause and consequence of poverty. Children of low-income families are less
likely to attend school than are their more affluent counterparts, since poor parents are less able to
purchase school supplies and because the labor of every household member is critical to the
survival of the family. In addition, low growth in the economy has depressed the job market in
recent years and many parents (especially those from socially-disadvantaged groups) question the
returns from investing in education. The situation is further exacerbated by limited access and
low quality of education, particularly in poorer and more backward regions. Current enrollment
figures indicate that not only are overall literacy levels lower in poorer regions of the state, but
current enrollments, particularly for girls and children from socially disadvantaged, are also
lower. This raises serious concerns about the persistence of poverty in the future.
Landlessness and poverty go hand in hand in India. Poverty levels fall sharply as average land
holdings increase. However, over half the rural poor own less than one-half hectare of land, and
much of what they own is of marginal quality. There is evidence that some poor households who
are slightly better off (e.g. the mobile poor) have gradually been acquiring land and are expanding
incomes through more intensive farming and livestock activities. And some are moving into
small-scale agro processing and sales of agriculture products. But for many of the poor,
acquiring more land is simply not an option; efforts by government to redistribute land have by
and large failed in Uttar Pradesh and few of the poor can hope to amass sufficient funds to
purchase land directly.
The poor in UP are realistic about the constraints and opportunities they face - including not only
economic constraints but social and political constraints as well. They realize the potential
importance of education in opening up economic opportunities, particularly in the non-farm
sector and in urban labor markets, and as well they realize the importance of land and other
productive assets. But they also see that investing in options with long term payoffs is risky and
uncertain and many are so severely constrained by the need to make ends meet each day that they
cannot afford to invest for future gains.
The poor in UP are highly vulnerable andface a range of serious risks and uncertainty. Low levels of
income, coupled with limited access to essential services and safety nets, make it particularly difficult
for the poor to weather unantcipated shocks. The destitute often became so due to idiosyncratic as
well as systemic shocks that have destroyed their earnings and asset base.
Very low levels of income, coupled with insecurity and inadequate access to formal and informal
safety nets, is the major cause of destitution in Uttar Pradesh. But even the non-destitute poor
suffer from a range of idiosyncratic shocks and remain highly vulnerable. For many of the poor,
the risks of downward mobility seem far more real than the likelihood of upward mobility and
escape from poverty. This is particularly true for the structural poor, with their limited access to
social networks of support and broader opportunities outside the local economy. While low
levels of income increase vulnerability, moderate increases in the incomes of the poor do not
always translate into reduced vulnerability. For example, recent studies have found that
unanticipated health "shocks" are a major cause of vulnerability, not only for the poor but for the
better-off as well. Children are particularly at risk, due in large part to poor sanitation, bad
drinking water, poor hygiene and health practices, and limited access to adequate health services.
Despite GOUP' s efforts to provide locally available primary health care for the rural poor, low
income households report high levels of expenditure on health care and treatment. Many public
facilities are understaffed and lack basic medicines and supplies. And government does not



Annex I
February 18,2000
Page 5 of 10
effectively regulate private sector providers who often supply services of dubious quality. In
short, in many regions adequate health care may not be available at any cost.
Concerns about violence, crime, and personal insecurity - particularly for women - are widely
acknowledged in Uttar Pradesh. Problems are particularly severe for the very poor and
individuals from socially-disadvantaged groups, who lack social status and political clout. Many
crimes go unreported, and there are numerous stories of abuse and mistreatment from police and
other local bodies. The poor are frequently victimized - by wealthy land owners, by government
officials, by thugs hired by the ruling elites. And they have little recourse to obtain justice -
grievance processes are slow and cumbersome in general, but particularly for the poor and
socially-disadvantaged. Justice is more often meted out on the street by local residents who
witness harassment or crimes than by the court system.
How do the poor protect themselves against vulnerability and risk? GOI supports a range of
formal safety net programs, including food subsidies, nutrition interventions, employment
assurance schemes, area development programs focused on basic infrastructure, and social
welfare programs. However, these programs do not operate effectively in many parts of Uttar
Pradesh. In fact, evidence suggests that formal safety nets and insurance mechanisms may be
least effective in regions where poverty is most pervasive and needs therefore greatest. When
illnesses or shocks occur, or even when dowry expenses of daughters must be met, poor
households frequently fall into chronic debt. High indebtedness is a factor that is linked to
vulnerability and to the inability of poor households to ultimately escape poverty. Low income
families have relatively few informal "social capital" relationships of mutual aid and support that
could provide emergency or longer-term relief in times of economic difficulty. They are,
therefore, the group most likely to rely upon money-lenders or members of upper-income
households for emergency assistance. This assistance often comes at high cost: members of
many indebted households are expected to labor in the fields or homes of creditor households for
wages well under market rates. In these cases, the ability of poor households to escape
indebtedness and its associated poverty is severely constrained. The real destitute often have
become so through idiosyncratic shocks: India's antipoverty programs and other formal safety
nets rarely deliver enough to ensure minimum subsistence needs are met.
NGOs have set up credit and savings schemes in some regions of the state that provide alternative
sources of funds for smoothing consumption and insuring against risk. Unfortunately coverage of
these schemes remains limited.
The poor have limited access to or influence over institutions of the state. Nor are they adequately
included in the political processes that ultimately deternine their lives.
There is widespread evidence that public spending in Uttar Pradesh is far more likely to benefit
the better off and politically powerful than the poor and socially disadvantaged. The poor take
this as a matter of course. Handpumps are located in wealthier neighborhoods; health clinics and
public hospitals are more responsive to the needs of their better off clients; access to schools and
the quality of education is far better in wealthier neighborhoods -- children from wealthier/less
socially advantaged household are more likely to be enrolled in schools, more likely to receive
special tutoring, and more likely to pass exams; development funds earmarked for antipoverty
and social welfare programs are frequently diverted to alternative uses - for example, grains
earmarked for the ICDS are siphoned off and sold in the private market, public employment
schemes are given to contractors who do not hire poor workers, over half of PDS food grains do
not even reach consumers, much less do they reach poor consumers.



Annex I
February 18,2000
Page 6 of 10
Better governance - more responsive, accountable, and transparent public institutions - is an essential
component for sustained poverty reduction in UP. The poor suffer most from corruption and inept
institutions at the local level, where services are actually provided. Governance reforms that start at the
top will trickle down slowly to lower tiers of government. Parallel reforms at the local level are essential
to serve the needs of the poor - the poor need stronger voice and political influence in local political
processes, and local institutions must be made structurally more responsive and accountable to all
citizens, not just the powerful and political elites.
Govermuent's Strategy for Reducing Poverty. GOUP's strategy for the reduction of poverty is
comprised of several mutually complementary initiatives - promotion of economic growth, expansion of
economic opportunities, improvement of access to basic services, and implementation of a series of
centrally-sponsored poverty alleviation schemes (Uttar Pradesh Ninth Five Year Plan, 1997-2002).
The slow down in the state's overall rate of growth in the 1990s, a result of poor growth in the
agricultural sector, appears to be a key factor behind UP's poor progress against rural poverty in recent
years. Between 1992-96, UP's primary sector grew only at 1.5 percent per annum. Declining capital
expenditure in physical and social infrastructure both in new investments and in O&M, and low rates of
capital formation in agriculture, and low productivity of existing investments, which are clearly linked to
the fiscal crisis and to poor management and govemance, are among the main causes of stagnant growth
and the current poverty situation. The share of capital expenditure as a proportion of total state
expenditure and as a proportion of GSDP have been declining since the early 1980s. Given the
persistence of a vicious cycle of high fiscal stress, low capital expenditure and low growth in the state,
even the modest target of reducing poverty by 7.3% over the course of the State's Ninth Five Year Plan
may be difficult to achieve.
The slowdown in agricultural growth rate in the 1990s has had particularly strong implications for
poverty reduction because UP's poor still derive the bulk of their income from the agriculture sector.
However, per worker productivity and wages are higher in the non-agricultural sector and vigorous
growth in this sector is a precondition for more rapid and sustained poverty reduction. Unfortunately UP
has not been able to attract sizable industrial investment in recent years, and there is little evidence of
labor force diversification (as found in many of the faster growing Indian states). In fact, official sources
indicate a deceleration in growth of secondary sector employment while agricultural employment
increased at a faster rate.4 There also has been a shift in the composition of the workforce towards casual
employment in the informal sector and employment in the organized sector has remained stagnant since
the mid 1980s.
In addition to growth, GOUP places great emphasis on so-called direct measures to reduce poverty.
These measures are designed both to protect the vulnerable segments of the population against
destabilizing shocks and to provide them with opportunities to assist them in their struggle to escape
poverty. They include (1) centrally sponsored antipoverty and social safety net schemes; (2) subsidies
and public support services, many justified on the basis of protecting the living standards of the poor; (3)
renewed efforts to improve human development, through increased and better delivery of basic services;
(4) recent efforts to expedite decentralization, as required under the 73rd and 74f amendments to the
constitution, and increase transparency and accountability at the local level. Recent work suggests that
many of these direct measures have been ineffective at reaching the poor, in large part due to weak
governance structures, mismanagement, and overly restrictive regulations that limit the capacity of the
programs to respond to local needs. Subsidies and support services often serve the interests to the better-
4These findings relate to the period 1981-91 for the Census, and 1987-88/1993-94 for the NSS (Singh 1998).



Annex I
February 18,2000
Page 7 of 10
off rather than the poor, and millions of poor people, particularly in southern and eastern regions of the
state, lack access to even the most basic of services.
Reforms and the Poor in Uttar Pradesh. In order to accelerate growth and improve economic
performance, GOUP is initiating a comprehensive program of reforms. While growth is a necessary
condition for sustained reductions in poverty, other measures are equally important to support poverty
reduction in its broader dimensions. The debate around economic reforms and poverty reduction often
focuses on potential adverse impacts of specific reform measures on the poor and other vulnerable
elements of society, particularly in the short-term. What is frequently overlooked is the wide range of
important positive benefits for the poor than can and indeed should flow from reform efforts in Uttar
Pradesh. Indeed, the success of the reform efforts in Uttar Pradesh must ultimately be judged by
achievements in terms of reducing and improving living conditions.
Fiscal reforms included under the program will enable a greater share of resources to be focused on high
priority expenditures -- for example, health and education services, as well as the basic infrastructure, so
critical for upward mobility and escape from poverty. Governance reforms should help to improve the
quality of public expenditures, and decentralization to local bodies create an enabling environment to
improve the quality of service delivery and enhance access of the poor to these services. Governance
reforms are particularly essential: priority areas for governance reforms linked to poverty reduction in UP
include (1) improvements in the delivery and quality of basic services such as health and education; (2)
more effective, better targeted anti-poverty programs and formal safety nets; (3) improving the regulatory
environment to promote private sector development and widen employment opportunities, particularly in
rural areas; (4) improving infrastructure (higher investments, better O&M) including roads,
communication, irrigation, drinking water and sanitation; (5) improving police and legislative functions to
promote greater personal security and effective dispute resolution.
Cost recovery measures in general, and more specifically increases in user charges for specific services,
that will be initiated as a part of sectoral reforms could increase the cost of key services to the poor and
other vulnerable groups, and thereby increase poverty. But user charges can be introduced selectively and
in such a way as to protect poor and vulnerable groups. And the potential benefits due to improved
availability and higher quality of services may far outweigh increases in the cost of services. Finally, the
distribution of public subsidies in high ticket items like power and education are enormously regressive:
while the poor pay relatively little, they get far less.
The question of how reforms will impact in practice on the poor and other vulnerable groups is complex.
Rather than devoting extensive resources to anticipating these impacts, a carefully designed Poverty and
Social Monitoring System (PSMS) has been developed to track actual changes in priority outcome
measures as well as key intermediate variables over the reform period. The PSMS thus provides a tool
that can be used by policy makers to monitor progress at achieving poverty and social objectives, identify
emerging positive as well as adverse impacts, and aid in the design of appropriate mitigation strategies.
UP Poverty and Social Monitoring System. The objectives of the Poverty and Social Monitoring
System (PSMS) are to
*  monitor progress in key areas related to poverty and living standards of the population in Uttar
Pradesh,
*  identify emerging problems with adverse impacts on the poor,
  keep public fully informed about progress as well as difficulties with regard to economic and
social issues, as well as
*  help GOUP in making more informed policy decisions.



Annex I
February 18,2000
Page 8 of 10
Data collected through the system is expected to be made available and used by a range of different
stakeholders -- government officials in various departments as well as local governments, special interest
groups, civil society organizations, the public at large, as well as the donor community so as to better
inform all concerned about poverty outcomes and impacts of key policy and program measures under the
state's reform program.
The system has three main components:
(i)    Indicators. Identification of a set of initial performance indicators that will be used to track
progress at reducing poverty and vulnerability and promoting human development. The
indicators have been developed drawing on the findings of the baseline study of key social and
economic factors responsible for poverty in Uttar Pradesh (see above) and consultations with a
wide range of stakeholders within the state, including consultations with the poor themselves.
(ii)    Monitoring. Implementation of a system of consistent, periodic data gathering for the core set of
performance indicators. The monitoring system will build capacity within GOUP to better use
existing statistical information as well as to collect new information. Survey-based as well as
participatory methods of data collection will be utilized.
(iii)   Reporting. Analysis of information emerging from the monitoring system to learn about
emerging trends in poverty, what is and is not working for the poor, and to inform policy
decisions.
Indicators. A detailed list of core indicators is included in Annex D. The indicators broadly map to the
findings of the baseline poverty assessment and diagnostic. In addition to standard measures of income
poverty, core indicators include measures linked to (a) wages and employment; reflecting the importance
of economic opportunities for poverty reduction; (b) education outcomes, including enrollment as well as
completion rates; (c) measures of health outcomes; (d) quality of housing services and access to basic
infrastructure such as water, sanitation, roads, and electricity; (e) access to and use of formal safety net
programs; (f) access to and quality of basic services, including but not limited to health and education; (g)
awareness of social rights and responsibilities. The information will be collected across a broad range of
households and communities in order to facilitate comparisons by social group, geographic region, etc.
And efforts will be made to quantify the core set of indicators as well as to collect a wider range of
qualitative indicators linked to such concerns as livelihood security, social inclusion/exclusion, security
and violence.
System of Data Collection. Indicators will be collected using a range of instruments over a five year
period. The instruments include: (i) a baseline household survey of a wide range of welfare indicators to
be implemented throughout the state in 1999-2000, and then repeated after 3-5 years, (ii) an ongoing
community surveillance system that monitors information on key quantitative indicators as well as a
range of qualitative indicators in regions and communities with a high incidence of poverty or considered
most at risk of adverse outcomes, (iii) additional focused surveys undertaken as needed to investigate
issues of special interest as they arise.
The baseline household survey constitutes the standard Government of India National Sample Survey
Organization Consumer Expenditure Survey questionnaire administered to a sample of approximately
14,000 households drawn from throughout the state. In addition to the standard questionnaire, a specially
designed poverty monitoring module has been developed and will be canvassed for the same set of
households. The new module collects data on access and use of essential services such as health,
education, sanitation, water, electricity, and use of government programs and services (anti-poverty
programs, credit, early childhood centers, etc.), main employment and income-earning activities, as well



Annex I
February 18,2000
Page 9 of 10
as asset ownership. Results from this survey will provide baseline values for tracking core monitoring
indicators, and the full survey will be repeated periodically over the course of the reforms.
The community survey is expected to be administered on an ongoing basis in a selected sample of 1000
communities in approximately 20 high priority districts located throughout the state. Changes in the
provision of infrastructure, delivery and quality of government programs and services, as well as other
intermediate indicators such as prices and wages will be monitored on a regular basis in these sentinel
villages to provide important supplementary information on changes in living conditions across the state.
Additional qualitative data collection exercises will also be launched to provide better information on risk
and vulnerability, changes in livelihood and coping strategies, as well as to reflect changes linked to
decentralization.
Additional surveys on topics of special interest will be developed and implemented over the course of the
project, as needs and issues arise. These might include (1) anticipating the impacts on specific groups of
specific policy measures and changes being considered by the government, (2) evaluation of particular
govemment programs or services, or (3) assessing the effects of unanticipated exogenous shocks or
adverse changes in the external environment. The additional surveys will be particularly useful in helping
the government investigate the effects of specific policy adjustments, as well as particular changes in the
economic environment on the poor, both from the point of view of learning as well as dissemination of
information to the wider public.
Institutional Arrangements and Reporting. The Poverty and Social Monitoring System will involve
coordinated effort from a number of different government departments and line agencies. Institutional
arrangements will play a crucial role in determining the overall success of the monitoring program.
The PSMS comprise three main sets of institutional actors; the specific roles of each are outlined below.
Under the system, it is proposed to separate the responsibility for data collection - which would be the
overall responsibility of the Department of Planning and Directorate of Economics and Statistics (DES) -
from the responsibility for reporting and policy analysis - which would be the primary responsibility of a
special unit set up the Chief Secretary's Branch. In addition, limited capacity would be developed in key
implementing departments - viz. Rural Development, SUDA, Rural Water and Sanitation, Panchyati Raj,
possibly others.-to track changes in the impact of essential antipoverty programs over the reform period,
drawing on information collected under the project.
(i) The Directorate of Economics and Statistics (DES) has an extensive network of field offices in all
districts throughout the state, and staff based in these offices will undertake most of the primary data
collection envisaged under the PSMS. All data will be consolidated at the DES head-office in Lucknow,
where a Poverty Data Unit has been established with responsibility for maintaining a comprehensive
computerized database of all the information collected. In addition, this unit will also be responsible for
servicing client needs by responding to requests for data and basic analysis, as well as for producing
standard reports on a regular basis.
In addition to the new data collection initiatives discussed above, some of the data already collected by
the Directorate of Economics and Statistics and other government departments will be computerized and
consolidated into the comprehensive database. There is tremendous variation in poverty incidence in Uttar
Pradesh, both regionally as well as among different groups. This data base will be maintained by DES at a
highly disaggregate level in order to track living conditions of different segments of society over time.
(ii) In addition to the Poverty Data Unit in DES, small monitoring cells will be set up in implementing
departments in GOUP with two specific objectives in mind. First, while implementing departments
typically track a range of physical indicators, including some input and output indicators, few have the



Annex I
February 18,2000
Page 10 of 10
capacity to evaluate the effectiveness of their programs at achieving final outcomes - viz., reducing
poverty and insecurity, improving nutrition, reducing ill-health. The PSMS will help to support and
further develop this capacity. Second, many departments initiate their own independent data collection
exercises rather than drawing on the services of DES, resulting in costly duplication and inefficient use of
scarce resources. Over the course of the PSMS DES will take on greater responsibility for servicing the
data needs of implementing departments, and coordinate these efforts through the monitoring cells.
(iii) Finally, the main demand for analytic work is likely to arise from the GOUP Chief Secretary's office
in response to issues and questions of strategic import. A Poverty Monitoring and Analysis Unit will be
set up under the Chief Secretary's office to provide leadership and ensure overall coordination of the
project. The specific responsibilities of the unit include (a) monitoring changes in key indicators on a
regular basis, (b) commissioning special-studies on selected topics of policy concern, (c) ensuring that the
information being collected and reports produced by the various organizations involved in the PSMS
match the needs of policy-makers, and (d) having primary responsibility for disseminating information
and responding to queries from the public at large.
Achievements. If implemented successfully, the Poverty and Social Monitoring System should achieve
the following:
(i)    establishing a computerized database of key poverty and social indicators that is maintained and
updated regularly by DES;
(ii)   creating an environment for better informed policy and program design within GOUP,
(iii)  improving the access of the public to key information, promoting greater public participation in
policy debates, encouraging greater transparency and public accountability; and ultimately,
(iv)   promoting a more pro-poor development strategy within the state.
